(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">CSO/Z-Spec</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1400\u20131500</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">9.9\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.6</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">mJy</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Conley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">CARMA</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">3400</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">0.61\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.19</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">mJy</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Conley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">VLA</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">214000</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">0.97\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.05</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">mJy</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Wardlow et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib159\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib159\">2013</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjaaabb7t3fna\" id=\"apjaaabb7t3fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>Total flux densities of the four lensed components, uncorrected for lensing magnification.\n<a name=\"apjaaabb7t3fnb\" id=\"apjaaabb7t3fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>Obtained by modeling the light profiles using <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>.\n<a name=\"apjaaabb7t3fnc\" id=\"apjaaabb7t3fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup>Expected <em>Spitzer</em>/IRAC fluxes of HLock01-B, extrapolated from the best-fit SED using flux measurements from 0.38 to 2.20 <em>\u03bc</em>m (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7s4\">4</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaaabb7t3fnd\" id=\"apjaaabb7t3fnd\"></a><sup> d</sup>Refers to the difference between the total flux densities measured in Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7app2\">B</a> (156, 188, 295, and 404 <em>\u03bc</em>Jy for the <em>Spitzer</em>/IRAC bands I1, I2, I3, and I4, respectively) and the expected flux densities of HLock01-B from the best-fit SED.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/suppdata/apjaaabb7t6_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/aaabb7\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/suppdata/apjaaabb7t6_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We followed Delvecchio et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib41\">2017</a>) and Miettinen et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib99\">2017</a>) to look for a possible AGN contribution in HLock01-R. We use the three-component fitting code <span class=\"small-caps\">Sed3fit</span> (Berta et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib6\">2013</a>), which accounts simultaneously for stellar, dust, and AGN emission. However, the stellar and dust components of <span class=\"small-caps\">Sed3fit</span> use the model libraries of da Cunha et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib32\">2008</a>), rather than the ones used in the new high-<em>z</em> extension of <span class=\"small-caps\">Magphys</span> (da Cunha et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib33\">2015</a>), which are expected to be better suited for high-<em>z</em> SMGs. We found a poor fit to our data with a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup>\u00a0=\u00a015.4 for the best-fit model, which is much higher than the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> of the standard <span class=\"small-caps\">Magphys</span> fit. Nevertheless, the mean AGN contribution to the total <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> was found to be 1%, and less than 20% in the IRAC bands. Our analysis does not completely exclude the possibility that HLock01-R may harbor an AGN (see also Conley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>; Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>; Scott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib135\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib135\">2011</a>; Magdis et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib88\">2014</a>, for a discussion of the presence of an AGN in HLock01), but its contribution to the total <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> is not substantial, as also pointed out by Rigopoulou et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib128\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib128\">2018</a>).</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f8\">8</a> shows our best-fit SED models for HLock01-B (<span class=\"small-caps\">Fast</span>) and HLock01-R (<span class=\"small-caps\">Magphys</span>). Our results show that both galaxies are undergoing simultaneous episodes of star formation activity (unobscured, nearly dust-free in HLock01-B, and dust-enshrouded star formation in HLock01-R), but they are physically very distinct. While HLock01-R is a very massive galaxy with an evolved stellar population, HLock01-B appears to be a young, lower-mass satellite of HLock01-R.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaabb7f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaabb7f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Best-fit model of the spectral energy distribution of HLock01-B (LBG, blue solid line) using <span class=\"small-caps\">Fast</span> (Kriek et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib76\">2009</a>), and HLock01-R (SMG, red solid line) using <span class=\"small-caps\">Magphys</span> (da Cunha et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib32\">2008</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib33\">2015</a>). The LBG fit uses photometry from CFHT <em>U</em> to WHT <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> bands, whereas for the SMG the fit uses photometry from the rest-frame UV (<em>HST</em> WFC3 F110W) to radio. We also used the estimated fluxes in the <em>Spitzer</em> bands (3.6, 4.5, 5.8, and 8.0 <em>\u03bc</em>m), as explained in the text. The unattenuated SED of the SMG is plotted as a red dashed line.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaabb7f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaabb7f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaabb7s5\" name=\"apjaaabb7s5\">5.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaaabb7s5-1\" name=\"apjaaabb7s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Close Merger or a Large Rotational Disk?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our high S/N GTC/OSIRIS spectrum of the optically bright lensed images of HLock01 (HLock01-B) shows several well-defined UV photospheric absorption lines, for which we secured the systemic redshift <em>z</em><sub>sys</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9546\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0004. This value differs by \u2212210 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> from the redshift of HLock01-R measured from the molecular gas lines <em>z</em><sub>CO</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1 0.0001 (Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>; Scott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib135\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib135\">2011</a>). A spatial offset of 3.3 kpc (in projection) has also been found in the source plane between the bulk of the stars that emit at rest-frame UV/optical wavelengths (HLock01-B), and the molecular gas and dust distribution associated with the luminous far-IR emitting source HLock01-R.</p><p>Although similar or even larger rotational velocities have been found in massive disk SMGs at high-<em>z</em> (e.g., Carilli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib21\">2010</a>; Daddi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib34\">2010</a>; Jim\u00e9nez-Andrade et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib70\">2017</a>; Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib71\">2017</a>), a scenario with HLock01-B being a dust-free region, that is part of a large rotational disk of HLock01-R, is unlikely. Such asymmetry in the dust distribution, with the lack of dust attenuation in HLock01-B, would be difficult to explain. Additionally, despite the large errors, the differences in the metallicity measured in the stars of HLock01-B and in the gas of the <em>Herschel</em> SMG HLock01-R (Rigopoulou et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib128\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib128\">2018</a>) suggest they are different galaxies with different enrichment histories.</p><p>Therefore, bringing together our GTC spectroscopic results and the complex velocity structure seen in the molecular gas reservoir in HLock01-R (Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>), we argue that HLock01 comprises two close but different sources, forming a pair of merging galaxies (HLock01-B and HLock01-R) separated by 3.3 kpc in projection. The merger scenario is also sustained by the broadness of the blueshifted ISM absorption lines seen in the spectrum of HLock01-B, suggesting highly turbulent gas likely produced by the close merger. While HLock01-R appears to be an evolved massive galaxy with a very large obscured star formation rate, HLock01-B is a young lower-mass satellite galaxy with photometric properties similar to those of LBGs, yet undergoing a young burst (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />6 Myr) of star formation, likely triggered by the gravitational interaction with the nearby massive SMG.</p><p>It is worth mentioning that without the gravitational lensing effect, our results could easily be mistaken. First, without the magnification in the apparent flux of HLock01-B, the systemic redshift, measured from faint stellar photospheric lines, would be difficult to obtain. In the absence of strong UV nebular emission lines, as is the case of HLock01-B, a significant continuum S/N is required to detect faint photospheric absorption lines. second, the projected 3.3 kpc spatial offset seen between the two different objects in the source plane would correspond to only 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4 without the lensing distortion, which is challenging to observe due to the limitation on the spatial resolution and sensitivity of current instruments.</p><p>It is thus no surprise that most high-<em>z</em> close mergers (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />10 kpc projected separation) have been discovered through the gravitational lensing effect (e.g., Ivison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib67\">2010b</a>; MacKenzie et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib85\">2014</a>; Messias et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib96\">2014</a>; Rawle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib117\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib117\">2014</a>; Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib162\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib162\">2014</a>; Spilker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib144\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib144\">2015</a>; Marrone et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib93\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib93\">2018</a>), with a few exceptions of unlensed, well separated, SMG\u2013SMG, SMG-QSO or SMG-LBG bright interacting pairs resolved with interferometric observations (e.g., Ivison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib63\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib66\">2008</a>; Smail et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib141\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib141\">2003</a>; Salom\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib131\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib131\">2012</a>; Oteo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib106\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib106\">2016</a>; Lu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib83\">2017</a>; Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib125\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib125\">2017</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaaabb7s5-2\" name=\"apjaaabb7s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0Outflow/Inflowing Gas</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Turning to the rest-frame UV spectral features, the internal kinematics of HLock01-B are very complex and different from what is observed in typical LBGs, showing two distinct components of the ISM. The blueshifted component of the ISM is centered at <em>v</em><sub>ISM</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0\u2212220 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> relative to the stars, which we associate with galaxy-scale outflows of material via stellar and supernova-driven winds, as seen in many other high-<em>z</em> star-forming galaxies (e.g., Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib138\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib138\">2003</a>; Steidel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib146\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib146\">2010</a>). This component is stronger (i.e., larger equivalent widths) in high-ionization lines, like Si <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> and C <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span>, similar to what is found in other young, low-metallicity galaxies (e.g., Erb et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib45\">2010</a>; James et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib69\">2014</a>). It is also detected in some low-ionization lines, but with a much broader profile (FWHM\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0900 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) than in typical LBGs (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />560 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>; Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib138\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib138\">2003</a>; Steidel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib146\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib146\">2010</a>), extending over a large velocity range from approximately \u22121000 to +600 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. We interpret that the broadness of the blueshifted absorption lines in HLock01-B is the result of a combination of strong winds of massive stars in the LBG and a complex velocity structure due to the close gravitational interaction with the massive SMG.</p><p>On the other hand, the redshifted component seen in all strong absorption lines (see Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f3\">3</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f4\">4</a>) is highly unusual and not seen in the many high-<em>z</em> galaxies studied (e.g., Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib138\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib138\">2003</a>; Steidel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib146\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib146\">2010</a>). This component can be understood as gas apparently moving toward the young stars of HLock01-B, because the absorbing gas must lie in front of the LBG. We relate this component with the large column density of foreground neutral gas (<em>N</em>(H <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>)\u00a0=\u00a0(5.83\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.24)\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>20</sup> cm<sup>\u22122</sup>), giving rise to the damped Ly<em>\u03b1</em> absorption seen in the spectrum of HLock01-B (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7s3-4\">3.4</a>). The detection of this absorption in both low- and high-ionization ISM lines also suggests that the gas has a broad range of temperatures, from cold, mostly neutral (e.g., O <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>), to warmer and ionized gas (e.g., Si <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span>, and C <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span>).</p><p>The origin and nature of the redshifted component seen in all strong absorption lines in the spectrum of HLock01-B is unclear, and with the available data we cannot arrive at a definite conclusion. Interpreting the redshifted component also depends strongly on the spatial location of the SMG and LBG along our line of sight. The relatively low dust attenuation in HLock01-B (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn25.gif\" alt=\"${A}_{{\\rm{V}}}={0.8}_{-0.3}^{+0.1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{A}_{{\\rm{V}}}={0.8}_{-0.3}^{+0.1}</script></span></span>) may suggest that the SMG is located in the background; otherwise the stellar continuum of the LBG would be highly attenuated by the foreground dust content of HLock01-R (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f7\">7</a>). Therefore it is unlikely that the redshifted component is associated with outflows from HLock01-R or rotating gas in its disk seen from the background HLock01-B.</p><p>In this sense, the redshifted component could be associated with a dwarf galaxy or a damped Ly<em>\u03b1</em> system falling toward HLock01. Gas ejected by a previous episode of star formation or AGN activity in HLock01 would not easily escape its intense gravitational pull. If cooled enough, these reservoirs of gas would provide additional fuel to prolong the star formation activity (e.g., Dav\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib39\">2011</a>; Hopkins et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib62\">2014</a>; Narayanan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib100\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib100\">2015</a>; Wang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib158\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib158\">2015</a>; Emonts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib44\">2016</a>). Assuming that the gas is dynamically linked and collapsing toward HLock01, it is more likely that the gas is falling into the massive SMG with <em>v</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0+170 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, instead of falling to HLock01-B with <em>v</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0+370 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, which seems too high for a 10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> galaxy.</p><p>Evidence of accretion of cool, metal-enriched gas has been found in only a few spectra of star-forming galaxies at moderately low-<em>z</em> (e.g., Sato et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib132\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib132\">2009</a>; Coil et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib29\">2011</a>; Martin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib94\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib94\">2012</a>; Rubin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib130\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib130\">2012</a>), and is more elusive at high-<em>z</em> (e.g., Bouch\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib11\">2013</a>; Wiseman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib160\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib160\">2017</a>) due to the faintness of individual high-<em>z</em> galaxies. Some authors suggest that the low detection rate of infalling gas is due to the geometry and alignment of the streams, which can only be detected in absorption if favorably aligned with our line of sight (Kimm et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib75\">2011</a>; Martin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib94\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib94\">2012</a>). Nevertheless, accretion of cold gas, either in the form of cold flows, mergers, or recycled gas from stellar feedback, plays an important role in star formation histories and galaxy growth.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaaabb7s5-3\" name=\"apjaaabb7s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Extended Gas Reservoir?</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We reported an unusual absorption line at <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />5281 \u00c5 in the red wing of a bright Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a03.327, associated with an object 14'' SW of HLock01 (see Appendix <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7app1\">A</a>). This absorption is consistent with C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb\u03bb</em>1334 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=2.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0008, very close by \u0394<em>v</em>\u00a0=\u00a0(\u22128\u00a0\u00b1\u00a076) km s<sup>\u22121</sup> to the redshift of HLock01-R measured from the molecular gas (<em>z</em><sub>CO</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0001). The limited spectral coverage of the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission line and the faintness of the continuum associated with the <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a03.327 galaxy does not let us unambiguously confirm if the absorption line is C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> at the redshift of HLock01-R, or a different absorption line system at a lower redshift. If related with HLock01-R, it may suggest a substantial gas reservoir in the halo at an impact parameter of 110 kpc. It is worth noting that Fu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib49\">2016</a>) used QSO absorption line spectroscopy in three high-<em>z</em> SMG-QSO close pairs, with the QSO at a larger redshift than the SMG, to probe the circumgalactic medium (CGM) at similar impact parameters. However, they did not find evidence of optically thick H <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> gas or strong neutral absorbers in the CGM. Our results suggest that at least massive SMGs, such as HLock01-R, may have prominent cool gas reservoirs in their halos that could fuel a prolonged star formation phase.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaaabb7s5-4\" name=\"apjaaabb7s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Physical Properties</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The intrinsic physical properties (corrected for lensing magnification) derived from multiband SED fitting reveal that HLock01-R is a far-IR luminous SMG (as already discussed in earlier papers; e.g., Conley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>; Wardlow et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib159\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib159\">2013</a>), with an ongoing SFR\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01500 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>, but yet heavily obscured at short wavelengths. Our new analysis reveals a large, highly obscured stellar mass, similar to the most massive and extreme SMGs during the peak of star formation (e.g., Hainline et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib58\">2011</a>; Ma et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib84\">2015</a>; Schinnerer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib133\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib133\">2016</a>; Miettinen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib99\">2017</a>; Nayyeri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib101\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib101\">2017</a>). However, as already discussed in Conley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>) and Wardlow et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib159\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib159\">2013</a>), HLock01-R has a moderately low <em>q</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.4 (which is the logarithmic ratio of <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> and the rest-frame 1.4 GHz flux density), compared with the mean value for HerMES sources (<em>q</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.40\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12; Ivison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib65\">2010a</a>). This may indicate a hidden, radio emitting AGN, but from our SED analysis in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7s4\">4</a>, we have shown that if HLock01-R harbors an AGN, its contribution to the total <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> and SFR is modest (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1%), as also noted by Rigopoulou et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib128\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib128\">2018</a>), and even assuming a maximum of 20% of an AGN contribution to the IRAC fluxes, the stellar mass of HLock01-R will be lower only by 0.1 dex, which is within our measurement errors. Moreover, Hayward &amp; Smith (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib60\">2015</a>) have also shown that the physical properties derived using <span class=\"small-caps\">Magphys</span> are robust even when the AGN contributes 25% of the total UV to IR luminosity. Our deep GTC/OSIRIS rest-frame UV spectroscopy does not show any line or continuum emission at the positions of the lensed images of HLock01-R, as some of them are included in the regions covered by our long-slit spectra (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f1\">1</a>, left panel). However, follow-up observations are required to constrain the presence of an AGN in HLock01-R. Nevertheless, even assuming a small AGN contribution, our results show that HLock01-R has already formed the majority of its stellar content, with a gas mass fraction of <em>f</em><sub>gas</sub> \u2261 <em>M</em><sub>gas</sub>/<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.07\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02 (for <em>M</em><sub>gas</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03.3\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, as measured in Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>), a specific star formation rate <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn26.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{sSFR}\\equiv \\mathrm{SFR}/{M}_{* }={2.76}_{-0.85}^{1.22}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{sSFR}\\equiv \\mathrm{SFR}/{M}_{* }={2.76}_{-0.85}^{1.22}</script></span></span> Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup>, and a depletion timescale (<em>\u03c4</em><sub>d</sub> \u2261 <em>M</em><sub>gas</sub>/SFR) of only 22 Myr (assuming no gas input). Moreover, it is plausible that additional gas input from the ongoing merger and inflows of material from a substantial gas reservoir in the halo will extend the starburst phase of HLock01 for a prolonged time, becoming an even more massive elliptical galaxy in the local universe.</p><p>On the other hand, HLock01-B appears to be a young, lower-stellar mass galaxy with very different properties than HLock01-R. In the optical, it is one of the brightest gravitationally lensed high-<em>z</em> star-forming galaxies known so far (e.g., Yee et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib163\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib163\">1996</a>; Allam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib2\">2007</a>; Belokurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib5\">2007</a>; Smail et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib142\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib142\">2007</a>; Lin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib81\">2009</a>; Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib161\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib161\">2010</a>; Bayliss et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib4\">2011</a>; Dahle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib36\">2016</a>; Marques-Chaves et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib92\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib92\">2017</a>), with an apparent total magnitude of <em>R</em>\u00a0=\u00a019.73\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01. Even after accounting for the magnification produced by the lensing group of galaxies (<em>\u03bc</em><sub><em>HST</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a08.5\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.5), it is still very luminous in the rest-frame UV with an absolute magnitude <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221223.4, two-and-a-half magnitudes more luminous than typical LBGs (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn27.gif\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span>) at a similar redshift (Reddy &amp; Steidel <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib120\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib120\">2009</a>). The stellar mass and SFR derived in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7s4\">4</a> yield a specific star formation rate of 55 Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup> well above the main sequence at that redshift (e.g., Mannucci et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib91\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib91\">2009</a>; Magdis et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib89\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib89\">2010b</a>; \u00c1lvarez-M\u00e1rquez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib3\">2016</a>). The nature and properties of this kind of UV ultraluminous galaxies (i.e., <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0\u221223) are still poorly understood, given the lack of examples reported in the literature (e.g., Allam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib2\">2007</a>; Bian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib9\">2012</a>; Le F\u00e8vre et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib78\">2013</a>; Marques-Chaves et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib92\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib92\">2017</a>; Ono et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib104\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib104\">2017</a>). This is due in part to the fact that this kind of galaxy is extremely rare, and finding them requires wider-field surveys with deep, multiband observations. The high UV luminosities also place these sources in the transition between luminous galaxies and faint AGNs, needing either extensive multi-wavelength imaging (e.g., X-ray, mid-IR, and radio) or spectroscopic follow-up. However, we stress that the unusual kinematics of the ISM and its high UV luminosity and SFR are not representative of the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 LBG population. Our results suggest that the gravitational interaction with the massive SMG may have triggered the larger UV luminosity and SFR. The interaction may also be the origin of the large obscured SFR and far-IR luminosity in the SMG. Despite this, HLock01-B shares many of its properties with the population of <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 LBGs. Its UV colors, (<em>G</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>R</em>)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.5 and (<em>U</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>G</em>)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01.4, are consistent with the standard color selection criteria of <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 LBGs (Steidel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib147\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib147\">1996</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib145\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib145\">2003</a>). However, HLock01-B presents (<em>R</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>K</em>)\u00a0=\u00a00.29\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.07, bluer than typical <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a03 LBGs ((<em>R</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>K</em>)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01.0; Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib137\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib137\">2001</a>), and Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emitting galaxies ((<em>R</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>K</em>)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.4; Ono et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib105\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib105\">2010</a>).</p><p>Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7t5\">5</a> summarizes the main physical properties of both components of HLock01.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaabb7s6\" name=\"apjaaabb7s6\">6.\u00a0Summary and Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have presented a detailed study of HLock01, one of the first gravitational lensed sources discovered in the HerMES survey. Unlike other SMGs, HLock01 is apparently very bright in all observed spectral bands, even in the optical. It is magnified by a factor of around 9 by a galaxy group-scale dark matter halo at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.645 and comprises four images in the observed plane. We have used OSIRIS on the GTC to secure a high S/N (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />30) rest-frame UV spectrum of the optically bright lensed images of HLock01, with an intermediate resolution (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />180 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>), covering the wavelength interval 1150\u20131950 \u00c5 in the rest-frame. From the analysis of these data together with other existing observations of HLock01, we arrive at the following main results.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We measured the systemic redshift of the optically bright lensed images of HLock01 (HLock01-B) <em>z</em><sub>sys</sub>\u00a0=2.9546\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0004 using weak stellar photospheric lines. This value is offset by \u2212210 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> from the redshift measured previously from the molecular gas lines <em>z</em><sub>CO</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0001 associated with the luminous far-IR source of HLock01 (HLock01-R). Our results show that the dust-obscured, far-IR emitting source HLock01-R, and the optically bright source HLock01-B, are most likely different galaxies undergoing a close merger or an interacting pair separated by only 3.3 kpc in projection.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We find a stellar metallicity for the stars in HLock01-B <em>Z</em><sub>stars</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.4 <em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub> based on two independent methods: blends of stellar photospheric lines and P-Cygni profiles from the most luminous O and B stars. This value differs slightly from that measured for the gas in HLock01-R (0.6\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>Z</em><sub>gas</sub>/<em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a01.0), based on far-IR fine-structure line ratios. A young (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />6 Myr) starburst model with a Salpeter IMF, stellar masses from 1 to 100 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, and an LMC/SMC metallicity explains well the properties of the high-ionization lines in HLock01-B.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The interstellar absorption lines in the spectrum of HLock01-B exhibit two distinct components. One is blueshifted by \u2212220 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> relative to the stars of HLock01-B, which we associate with galaxy-scale outflows via stellar and supernovae driven winds. However, it also shows a broader profile (FWHM\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0900 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) than in most star-forming galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02\u20133, indicating highly turbulent kinematics of the outflowing gas likely due to the close merger. This component is stronger in high-ionization lines, suggesting that the gas is mostly ionized or the neutral gas has a lower covering factor than the ionized gas. Another absorption component is seen in the spectrum of HLock01-B, but is redshifted relative to either HLock01-B and HLock01-R by +370 and +170 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, respectively, which can be understood as gas moving toward both galaxies. We relate this component with the strong damped Ly<em>\u03b1</em> line seen in HLock01-B, with a column density of <em>N</em>(H <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>)\u00a0=\u00a0(5.83\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.24)\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>20</sup> cm<sup>\u22122</sup>. Although with the available data we cannot arrive at a definitive conclusion on its nature and origin, we interpret this absorption feature as gas falling toward HLock01-R, which is more massive, with <em>v</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0170 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, but viewed in absorption along a favorable line of sight toward HLock01-B. This component is detected in both low- and high-ionization interstellar lines, but with slightly different absorption line profiles, suggesting that the gas has a broad range of temperatures, and possibly different origin.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We detected an unusual absorption line in the red wing of the bright Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a03.327 at 14'' SW from the lensing galaxy G1, which we tentatively associate with C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb\u03bb</em>1334 at the redshift of HLock01-R. If this absorption is related with HLock01-R and not with an absorbing system at a different redshift, it indicates a substantial gas reservoir in the halo of HLock01 at a projected distance of 110 kpc. Additionally, we report a broad absorption line QSO at a projected distance of 2.5 Mpc from HLock01, with a redshift very close to HLock01-R (\u0394<em>v</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>).</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Our revised SED fitting with two different galaxies, one very bright in the optical and the other in the far-IR, implies that both are physically very distinct. HLock01-B appears to be a young, lower-mass satellite galaxy of HLock01-R, undergoing an intense episode of star formation activity likely triggered by the interaction. HLock01-R shows an already evolved stellar population, and its high stellar mass in combination with the low gas fraction suggests that the SMG has already assembled most of its stellar mass. However, additional gas input from the satellite galaxy HLock01-B and from the reservoir of gas around HLock01-R may extend the starburst phase of the SMG, eventually forming one of the most massive galaxies in the local universe.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We would like to thank the anonymous referee for their suggestions, which significantly improved the clarity of this paper. We also thank Alice Shapley for allowing us to use their rest-frame UV composite spectrum of LBGs and Helmut Dannerbauer for useful discussions. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f9\">9</a> shows the profiles of the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission, as well as the coordinates and magnitudes of the associated objects seen in CFHT <em>R</em> band data.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaabb7f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaabb7f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0CFHT <em>R</em> band showing the spatial distribution of the <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a02.96 BAL QSO SDSS J105715.48+573324.3 (top right corner, green dashed square), and three high-redshift galaxies (LAEs) serendipitously detected in two of our GTC long-slit spectra (red dashed squares). The position of HLock01 is marked in blue. The redshift of the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> lines, as well as the coordinates and <em>R</em> band magnitudes of the associated objects, are labeled in each panel. In particular, the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.327 Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission (bottom right corner) shows an unresolved absorption line at 5281 \u00c5 in its red wing. This absorption is consistent with the low-ionization line C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb\u03bb</em>1334 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0008 (inset panel), very close (\u0394<em>v</em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u22128\u00a0\u00b1\u00a076 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) to the redshift of HLock01-R measured from the molecular gas (<em>z</em><sub>CO</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0001; Riechers et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>; Scott et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib135\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib135\">2011</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaabb7f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaabb7f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In particular, the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.327 shows a broad profile (FWHM\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01000 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, after accounting for the instrumental broadening), and has an observed flux of <em>F</em><sub>Ly<em>\u03b1</em></sub>\u00a0=(1.38\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2)\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>\u221215</sup> erg s<sup>\u22121</sup> cm<sup>\u22122</sup>. A more detailed analysis of this object will be presented in R. Marques-Chaves et al. (2018, in preparation), based on recent observations with GTC. We noticed that the red wing of the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission shows an unusual and unresolved (FWHM\u00a0&lt;\u00a0180 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) absorption line at 5281.2\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.9 \u00c5 (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f9\">9</a>, bottom right corner), which is not related to this galaxy. This could be an absorption system at any lower redshift, but surprisingly it is consistent with C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb\u03bb</em>1334 absorption at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0008, very close (\u0394<em>v</em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u22128\u00a0\u00b1\u00a076 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) to the redshift of HLock01-R measured from the molecular gas (<em>z</em><sub>CO</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.9574\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0001; Riechers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib124\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib124\">2011</a>; Scott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib135\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib135\">2011</a>). If this absorption is physically related to HLock01, it may suggest a substantial gas reservoir in its halo, at an impact parameter <em>b</em>\u00a0=\u00a0110 kpc.</p><p>We also report on a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.961 quasar, SDSS J105715.48+573324.3, at 5<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcm.gif\" alt=\"farcm\" align=\"baseline\" />5 NW from HLock01 (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f9\">9</a> at the top right corner). This object was cataloged as a broad absorption line quasar (BAL QSO) by Trump et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib156\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib156\">2006</a>) from the third edition of the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib164\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib164\">2000</a>) Quasar Catalog (Schneider et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib134\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib134\">2005</a>). The redshift of this BAL QSO is very close to the one of HLock01-R (\u0394<em>v</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) and is located at a projected distance of 2.5 Mpc.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaabb7app2\" name=\"apjaaabb7app2\">Appendix B: Broadband Photometry</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In addition to the photometry presented in Conley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib30\">2011</a>) and Wardlow et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib159\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib159\">2013</a>), we also performed photometry on the new imaging data. These measurements are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7t6\">6</a>.</p><p>We use aperture photometry in the <em>U</em> band from the corresponding CFHT/MEGACAM catalog, which contains detections of all four lensed images, since the light contamination from the red, lensing galaxies is negligible in <em>U</em> band. For GTC <em>g</em> band, we applied aperture photometry on the lensed images A, C, and D. We exclude photometry of the lensed image B, as it is strongly blended with the lens galaxy G4, but we use the lens model to correct for the omitted light from image B (a roughly 15% correction).</p><p>Despite the short exposure time of the <em>HST</em> F110W data, faint emission is seen close to the radio and submm lensed images A and C (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f10\">10</a>). <em>K</em> band imaging from WHT/LIRIS and NIRC2/Keck-II (the latter discussed in Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>; Calanog et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib17\">2014</a>) also reveal faint emission at these positions. The red colors from the optical to 2.2 <em>\u03bc</em>m imaging seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f10\">10</a> (left panel) and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7t6\">6</a> suggest that this faint emission corresponds to the obscured rest-frame UV and optical light of the <em>Herschel</em> SMG. Associating this faint emission to HLock01-R, we measure the flux in a small aperture (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />77 diameter) at the position of the faint near-IR source detected close to the radio and submm lensed image C. For HLock01-B, we use larger apertures (2''\u20133'') on the lensed images A, C, and D, and then subtract the contribution of HLock01-R, which in any case is less than 5%. The lens model was used again to add the light from the lensed image B.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaabb7f10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaabb7f10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a04''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a04''\u00a0cutouts of the lensed images A (upper panels) and C (lower panels) of HLock01. From left to right, we show a <em>g</em>, <em>I</em>, and <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> color image; high-resolution <em>HST</em>/WFC3 F110W; near-IR WHT/<em>K</em><sub>s</sub>; <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model of the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> data; and finally the resulting residuals after subtracting the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model. 1.4 GHz VLA contours are in orange and correspond to the positions of two of the lensed images of the <em>Herschel</em> SMG. Blue and red crosses mark, respectively, the positions of the LBG and the SMG in our <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaabb7f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaabb7f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Near-IR WHT/<em>K</em><sub>s</sub> photometry of the individual components (SMG and LBG of HLock01) was obtained after modeling the light distribution of each component in the lensed images A and C, using the two-dimensional fitting program <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> (Peng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib111\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib111\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib112\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib112\">2010</a>). We use S\u00e9rsic profiles centered at the centroids of the <em>HST</em>/F110W emission, allowing only one pixel freedom (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />254). A nearby star was chosen as a point-spread function (PSF) model. Note that we only perform the fit in the lensed images A and C, the only ones that show detections of the faint, obscured counterparts of the SMG.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7fn8\">24</a></sup>\n The lens model of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>) was used again to add the light from the lensed images B and C. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f10\">10</a> shows our <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model, as well as the resulting residuals after subtracting the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> model.</p><p>This field has been observed by the SWIRE survey (Lonsdale et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib82\">2003</a>) in the cryogenic phase of <em>Spitzer</em> and to deeper levels in the two first bands of IRAC (3.6 and 4.5 <em>\u03bc</em>m) by the SERVS survey (Mauduit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib95\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib95\">2012</a>) in the post-cryogenic phase. In the <em>Spitzer</em> Enhanced Imaging Products (SEIP) catalog<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaaabb7fn9\">25</a></sup>\n of this area (based on the SWIRE data), the individual lensed images A and C are the only ones resolved and detected. However, their fluxes appear relatively large and inconsistent (<em>f</em><sub>A</sub>/<em>f</em><sub>C</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01) with the expected values from the individual magnifications provided by the lens model of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011; </a><em>f</em><sub>A</sub>/<em>f</em><sub>C</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01.7). Given the limited spatial resolution of IRAC (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />2''), the cataloged fluxes of the lensed images A and C are likely contaminated by foreground light, mainly due to the G1, G4, and G6 lensing galaxies. To perform better photometry on HLock01, we use <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> to model the light distribution of both foreground and background components in the SERVS images. We use S\u00e9rsic profiles centered at the positions of the detected <em>HST</em>/F110W counterparts, and a nearby star was chosen as a PSF model. We then measure the flux density of our best-fit model of the lensed images A and C, and use the lens model to add the expected light from the other lensed images to obtain the total observed flux. Modeling the light distribution using <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> to separate the fluxes from the SMG and the LBG does not help and will introduce significant uncertainties in their measurements, since the spatial separation of the SMG and the LBG in the lensed images A and C is substantially lower (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9) than the intrinsic PSF in IRAC data (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />2'' FWHM). Finally, in the 8.0\u00a0<em>\u03bc</em>m IRAC band, the foreground light contamination appears to be much lower than in the other IRAC bands; thus we use the 3<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8 aperture photometry for the lensed images A and C provided in the SEIP catalog, with the appropriate aperture corrections. Again, we use the lens model to add the expected light of the lensed images B and D.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaaabb7app3\" name=\"apjaaabb7app3\">Appendix C: Lens Modeling</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We use the <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 FWHM <em>HST</em>/F110W image data to update the lens model already described in Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>). The procedure is identical and uses the dedicated code <span class=\"small-caps\">sl</span>_<span class=\"small-caps\">fit</span> (for more details, see also Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib53\">2007</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib51\">2008</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib52\">2012</a>). We fit model parameters of simple analytical lensing potentials and model background galaxies as simple elliptical S\u00e9rsic profiles. The lensing potential is primarily constrained by the <em>HST</em> data, with the highest resolution and S/N. The mass distribution is then held fixed in order to fit for the parameters defining the light distribution in the other channels. We assume the deflector to be made of an isothermal elliptical mass distribution centered on the galaxy G1, and we also include the perturbing galaxies G2, G3, and G4 as point masses centered on the substructure light emission. We allow for the presence of a core radius that softens the inner mass distribution in each case. Unlike in Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>), we do not place masses at G5 and G6, since they have a negligible impact on the mass model and their masses are essentially unconstrained. G1, being by far the most massive galaxy in the vicinity, is assumed to be at the center of the group-scale total mass distribution. The collective effect of a few possible perturbing galaxies 10''\u201320'' south of G1 may induce some external shear, which will contribute to the quadrupole of the mass distribution, but having too few constraints spanning too small a radial range around G1, we assume that the ellipticity of the mass distribution centered on G1 will absorb the total quadrupole. Higher order effects (like <em>m</em> = 1 or <em>m</em> = 3 multipoles) would also be hard to constrain with the current data.</p><p>The lensed features exhibiting a cross-like (or barely fold-like) configuration requires a source relatively close to the optical axis, and thus, relatively far for a widely opened main astroid caustic. We do not expect much magnification nor huge spatial variations of the magnification over the extent of the source. This contrasts with a cusp configuration (see, e.g., the cluster MS 0451.6-0305 in MacKenzie et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib85\">2014</a>).</p><p>Modeling the <em>HST</em> F110W data first, we assume the background source is made of one single elliptical exponential profile for which we adjust source plane position, ellipticity, orientation, effective radius, and flux. Before fitting the lensed light emission, we performed a fit to the foreground light emission (i.e., G1\u2013G5) in order to subtract it off. The result is shown in the top row panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f11\">11</a>. The formal uncertainty on the recovered Einstein radius (i.e., lens amplitude) is unrealistically small (~0.2% relative), given the high S/N of the widely extended lensed images. However, large-scale structure mass fluctuations along the line of sight, as well as unaccounted for substructures in the lensing mass distribution, should place a lower limit of order 1%\u20132% on the accuracy to which the Einstein radius can be measured. By artificially increasing the pixel rms errors in the F110W imaging data by a factor of 10, we are able to mimic this additional source of noise. As a result, we achieve a 1% accuracy on the recovered Einstein radius as 4<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />08 \u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />05, consistent with the previous model of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>). The core radius is found to be 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 \u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1, and the mass distribution is very elongated, with an axis ratio <em>b</em>/<em>a</em> = 0.38 \u00b1 0.03. The mass of perturbing galaxies is poorly constrained: <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn28.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{{\\rm{G}}2}=({6.0}_{-4.3}^{+5.1})\\,\\times {10}^{10}\\,{\\text{}}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{{\\rm{G}}2}=({6.0}_{-4.3}^{+5.1})\\,\\times {10}^{10}\\,{\\text{}}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, <em>M</em><sub>G3</sub>\u00a0\u2264\u00a04.4\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, except the case of G4, which induces a splitting of one of the multiple images (B1\u2013B2), yielding <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn29.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{{\\rm{G}}4}=({9.1}_{-2.6}^{+6.0})\\times {10}^{10}\\,{\\text{}}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{{\\rm{G}}4}=({9.1}_{-2.6}^{+6.0})\\times {10}^{10}\\,{\\text{}}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>. We find a total magnification <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0=\u00a08.5\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.5 in the <em>HST</em> F110W band. The formal errors on magnification do not account for the mass-sheet degeneracy (related to a strong assumption about the mass density slope), which is responsible for the differences with the magnification we reported in Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>). Channel-to-channel differential magnifications are, on the other hand, more robust and do not depend on the assumed mass distribution. From the best-fit mass model inferred from <em>HST</em> F110W data, we extract for the position, ellipticity, size, and flux of an exponential disk in the GTC, VLA, PdBI CO <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn30.gif\" alt=\"$(J=5\\to 4$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">(J=5\\to 4</script></span></span>), and SMA continuum data. The output of the modeling in all the channels is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaaabb7f11\">11</a>. From top to bottom, each row represents the results of <em>HST</em> F110W, GTC <em>g</em> band, 880 <em>\u03bc</em>m SMA, PdBI CO <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn31.gif\" alt=\"$(J=5\\to 4$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">(J=5\\to 4</script></span></span>), and VLA 1.4 GHz observations. The overall aspect does not change much with respect to the model of Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaaabb7bib50\">2011</a>). The geometry of the source at long wavelengths is poorly determined, and the elongated shape of the best-fit sources in the lower panels is not significant.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaaabb7f11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaaabb7f11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f11_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 11.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 11.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7f11_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 11.</strong>\u00a0Lens inversion results in several bands. Top to bottom: <em>HST</em> F110W; GTC <em>g</em> band; 880\u00a0<em>\u03bc</em>m SMA; PdBI CO <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/854/2/151/Full/apjaaabb7ieqn32.gif\" alt=\"$(J=5\\to 4$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">(J=5\\to 4</script></span></span>); and VLA 1.4 GHz observations. From left to right: input image (foreground deflectors are preliminarily subtracted off for <em>HST</em>); reconstructed image plane model; residual of input\u2014reconstructed image; and finally the source plane reconstruction. All the images are centered on the lensing galaxy G1 (<em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a0+10:57:50.959, <em>\u03b4</em>\u00a0=\u00a0+57:30:25.66, J2000) and oriented such that north is up and east is to the left. At long wavelengths, the limited spatial resolution induces large fluctuations in the shown reconstructed maximum a posteriori sources (right panels), which can get very elongated (see Figure <a href=\"#apjaaabb7f7\">7</a> for a comparison of more realistic posterior mean sources).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaaabb7f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaaabb7f11\" href=\"/0004-637X/854/2/151/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaaabb7f11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list wd-content-footnotes\">\n\t\t\t\t<li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaabb7fn1\" id=\"apjaaabb7fn1\">17\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaabb7fn4\" id=\"apjaaabb7fn4\">20\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/SERVS/\">http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/SERVS/</a>\n</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaabb7fn6\" id=\"apjaaabb7fn6\">22\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>We test if some of these lines are saturated by considering the linear part of the curve of growth. In this case, the ratios of the rest-frame equivalent widths (EW<sub>0</sub>) of different transitions of a given ion can be related through their oscillator strengths. For example, for the Si ii lines in the unsaturated case, we would expect EW<sub>0</sub> (1260)/EW<sub>0</sub> (1526)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a05, EW<sub>0</sub> (1260)/EW<sub>0</sub> (1304)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010, and EW<sub>0</sub> (1304)/EW<sub>0</sub> (1526)\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a00.5. Our spectrum shows ratios of <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />1.0, 2.5, and 0.4, respectively, suggesting that at least Si ii <em>\u03bb\u03bb</em> 1260 may be saturated.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apjaaabb7fn7\" id=\"apjaaabb7fn7\">23\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" id=\"apjaaabb7fn8\">24\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>This can be explained through the lensed images A and C being less affected by foreground contamination, and their lensing magnifications are higher than the ones of images B and D (see Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaaabb7bib50\"
